Chapter 1
COMPLEX NUMBERS
1.1.
Arithmetic and Conjugates
The purpose of this chapter is to give a review of various properties of the complex numbers that we shall
need in the discussion of complex analysis. As the reader is expected to be familiar with the material,
all proofs have been omitted.
The equation x2 +1 = 0 has no solution x âˆˆR. To â€œsolveâ€ this equation, we have to introduce extra
numbers into our number system. To do this, we deï¬ne the number i by i2 + 1 = 0, and then extend the
ï¬eld of all real numbers by adjoining the number i, which is then combined with the real numbers by the
operations addition and multiplication in accordance with the Field axioms of the real number system.
The numbers a + ib, where a, b âˆˆR, of the extended ï¬eld are then added and multiplied in accordance
with the Field axioms, suitably extended, and the restriction i2 + 1 = 0. Note that the number a + 0i,
where a âˆˆR, behaves like the real number a.
What we have said in the last paragraph basically amounts to the following. Consider two complex
numbers a + ib and c + id, where a, b, c, d âˆˆR. We have the addition and multiplication rules
(a + ib) + (c + id) = (a + c) + i(b + d)
and
(a + ib)(c + id) = (ac âˆ’bd) + i(ad + bc).
These lead to the subtraction rule
(a + ib) âˆ’(c + id) = (a âˆ’c) + i(b âˆ’d),
and the division rule, that if c + id Ì¸= 0, then

Chapter 2
FOUNDATIONS OF COMPLEX ANALYSIS
2.1.
Three Approaches
We start by remarking that analysis is sometimes known as the study of the four Câ€™s: convergence,
continuity, compactness and connectedness. In real analysis, we have studied convergence and continuity
to some depth, but the other two concepts have been somewhat disguised. In this course, we shall try
to illustrate these two latter concepts a little bit more, particularly connectedness.
Complex analysis is the study of complex valued functions of complex variables. Here we shall
restrict the number of variables to one, and study complex valued functions of one complex variable.
Unless otherwise stated, all functions in these notes are of the form f : S â†’C, where S is a set in C.
We shall study the behaviour of such functions using three diï¬€erent approaches. The ï¬rst of these,
discussed in Chapter 3 and usually attributed to Riemann, is based on diï¬€erentiation and involves pairs
of partial diï¬€erential equations called the Cauchy-Riemann equations. The second approach, discussed in
Chapters 4â€“11 and usually attributed to Cauchy, is based on integration and depends on a fundamental
theorem known nowadays as Cauchyâ€™s integral theorem. The third approach, discussed in Chapter 16
and usually attributed to Weierstrass, is based on the theory of power series.

properties of the point sets S play a crucial role in our study. We therefore begin by discussing various
types of point sets in the complex plane.

subsequent discussion.

2.3.
Complex Functions
In these lectures, we study complex valued functions of one complex variable. In other words, we study
functions of the form f : S â†’C, where S is a set in C. Occasionally, we will abuse notation and simply
refer to a function by its formula, without explicitly deï¬ning the domain S. For instance, when we
discuss the function f(z) = 1/z, we implicitly choose a set S which will not include the point z = 0
where the function is not deï¬ned. Also, we may occasionally wish to include the point z = âˆin the
domain or codomain.
We may separate the independent variable z as well as the dependent variable w = f(z) into real
and imaginary parts. Our usual notation will be to write z = x + iy and w = f(z) = u + iv, where
x, y, u, v âˆˆR. It follows that u = u(x, y) and v = v(x, y) can be interpreted as real valued functions of
the two real variables x and y.
Example 2.3.1.
Consider the function f : S â†’C, given by f(z) = z2 and where S = {z âˆˆC : |z| < 2}
is the open disc with radius 2 and centre 0. Using polar coordinates, it is easy to see that the range of
the function is the open disc f(S) = {w âˆˆC : |w| < 4} with radius 4 and centre 0.
Example 2.3.2.

half-plane and f(z) = z2. Using polar coordinates, it is easy to see that the range of the function is the
complex plane minus the non-negative real axis.

2.4.
Extended Complex Plane
It is sometimes useful to extend the complex plane C by the introduction of the point âˆat inï¬nity. Its
connection with ï¬nite complex numbers can be established by setting z + âˆ= âˆ+ z = âˆfor all z âˆˆC,
and setting z Â· âˆ= âˆÂ· z = âˆfor all non-zero z âˆˆC. We can also write âˆÂ· âˆ= âˆ.
Note that it is not possible to deï¬ne âˆ+ âˆand 0 Â· âˆwithout violating the laws of arithmetic.
However, by special convention, we shall write z/0 = âˆfor z Ì¸= 0 and z/âˆ= 0 for z Ì¸= âˆ.
In the complex plane C, there is no room for a point corresponding to âˆ. We can, of course,
introduce an â€œidealâ€ point which we call the point at inï¬nity. The points in C, together with the point
at inï¬nity, form the extended complex plane. We decree that every straight line on the complex plane
shall pass through the point at inï¬nity, and that no half-plane shall contain the ideal point.
The main purpose of this section is to introduce a geometric model in which each point of the
extended complex plane has a concrete representative. To do this, we shall use the idea of stereographic
projection.
Consider a sphere of radius 1 in R3. A typical point on this sphere will be denoted by P(x1, x2, x3).
Note that x2
1 + x2
2 + x2
3 = 1. Let us call the point N(0, 0, 1) the north pole. The equator of this sphere is
the set of all points of the form (x1, x2, 0), where x2
1 + x2
2 = 1. Consider next the complex plane C. This
can be viewed as a plane in R3. Let us position this plane in such a way that the equator of the sphere
lies on this plane; in other words, our copy of the complex plane is â€œhorizontalâ€ and passes through the
origin. We can further insist that the x-direction on our complex plane is the same as the x1-direction
in R3, and that the y-direction on our complex plane is the same as the x2-direction in R3. Clearly a
typical point z = x + iy on our complex plane C can be identiï¬ed with the point Z(x, y, 0) in R3.

Suppose that Z(x, y, 0) is on the plane. Consider the straight line that passes through Z and the
north pole N. It is not too diï¬ƒcult to see that this straight line intersects the surface of the sphere at
precisely one other point P(x1, x2, x3). In fact, if Z is on the equator of the sphere, then P = Z. If Z is
on the part of the plane outside the sphere, then P is on the northern hemisphere, but is not the north
pole N. If Z is on the part of the plane inside the sphere, then P is on the southern hemisphere. Check
that for Z(0, 0, 0), the point P(0, 0, âˆ’1) is the south pole.
On the other hand, if P is any point on the sphere diï¬€erent from the north pole N, then a straight
line passing through P and N intersects the plane at precisely one point Z. It follows that there is a
pairing of all the points P on the sphere diï¬€erent from the north pole N and all the points on the plane.
This pairing is governed by the requirement that the straight line through any pair must pass through
the north pole N.
We can now visualize the north pole N as the point on the sphere corresponding to the point at
inï¬nity of the plane. The sphere is called the Riemann sphere.
2.5.
Limits and Continuity
The concept of a limit in complex analysis is exactly the same as in real analysis. So, for example, we
say that f(z) â†’L as z â†’z0, or
lim
zâ†’z0 f(z) = L,
if, given any Ïµ > 0, there exists Î´ > 0 such that |f(z) âˆ’L| < Ïµ whenever 0 < |z âˆ’z0| < Î´.
This deï¬nition will be perfectly in order if the function f is deï¬ned in some open set containing
z0, with the possible exception of z0 itself. It follows that if z0 is an interior point of the region S of
deï¬nition of the function, our deï¬nition is in order. However, if z0 is a boundary point of the region S
of deï¬nition of the function, then we agree that the conclusion |f(z) âˆ’L| < Ïµ need only hold for those
z âˆˆS satisfying 0 < |z âˆ’z0| < Î´.
Similarly, we say that a function f(z) is continuous at z0 if f(z) â†’f(z0) as z â†’z0. A similar
qualiï¬cation on z applies if z0 is a boundary point of the region S of deï¬nition of the function. We also
say that a function is continuous in a region if it is continuous at every point of the region.

Note that for a function to be continuous in a region, it is enough to have continuity at every point of
the region. Hence the choice of Î´ may depend on a point z0 in question. If Î´ can be chosen independently
of z0, then we have some uniformity as well. To be precise, we make the following deï¬nition.
Definition.
A function f(z) is said to be uniformly continuous in a region S if, given any Ïµ > 0, there
exists Î´ > 0 such that |f(z1) âˆ’f(z2)| < Ïµ for every z1, z2 âˆˆS satisfying |z1 âˆ’z2| < Î´.
Remark.
Note that if we ï¬x z2 to be a point z0 and write z for z1, then we require |f(z) âˆ’f(z0)| < Ïµ
for every z âˆˆS satisfying |z âˆ’z0| < Î´. In other words, Î´ cannot depend on z0.

3.3.
Analytic Functions
In the previous section, we have shown that diï¬€erentiability in complex analysis leads to a pair of partial
diï¬€erential equations. Now partial diï¬€erential equations are seldom of interest at a single point, but
rather in a region. It therefore seems reasonable to make the following deï¬nition.
Definition.
A function f is said to be analytic at a point z0 âˆˆC if it is diï¬€erentiable at every z in
some Ïµ-neighbourhood of the point z0. The function f is said to be analytic in a region if it is analytic
at every point in the region. The function f is said to be entire if it is analytic in C.
Example 3.3.1.
Consider the function f(z) = |z|2. In our usual notation, we clearly have
u = x2 + y2
and
v = 0.
The Cauchy-Riemann equations
2x = 0
and
2y = 0
can only be satisï¬ed at z = 0. It follows that the function is diï¬€erentiable only at the point z = 0, and
is therefore analytic nowhere.
Example 3.3.2.
The function f(z) = z2 is entire.
Example 3.3.3.
Suppose that the function f is analytic in a domain D. Suppose further that f has
constant real part u. Then clearly
âˆ‚u
âˆ‚x = 0
and
âˆ‚u
âˆ‚y = 0.
Since f is analytic in D, it is diï¬€erentiable at every point in D, and so the Cauchy-Riemann equations
hold in D. It follows that
âˆ‚v
âˆ‚x = 0
and
âˆ‚v
âˆ‚y = 0.
Hence f must have constant imaginary part v, and so f must be constant in D.
Example 3.3.4.
Suppose that the function f is analytic in a domain D. Suppose further that f has
constant imaginary part v. A similar argument shows that f must have constant real part u. Hence f
must be constant in D.
Example 3.3.5.

constant modulus. In other words, u2 + v2 = C for some non-negative real number C. Diï¬€erentiating

In this section, we shall generalize various functions that we have studied in real analysis to the complex

3.5.
Periodicity and its Consequences
One of the fundamental diï¬€erences between real and complex analysis is that the exponential function
is periodic in C.
Definition.
A function f is periodic in C if there is some ï¬xed non-zero Ï‰ âˆˆC such that the identity
f(z + Ï‰) = f(z) holds for every z âˆˆC. Any constant Ï‰ âˆˆC with this property is called a period of f.
THEOREM 3C.
The exponential function ez is periodic in C with period 2Ï€i. Furthermore, any
period Ï‰ âˆˆC of ez is of the form Ï‰ = 2Ï€ki, where k âˆˆZ is non-zero.
Proof.
The ï¬rst assertion follows easily from the observation
e2Ï€i = cos 2Ï€ + i sin 2Ï€ = 1.
Suppose now that Ï‰ âˆˆC. Clearly ez+Ï‰ = ez implies eÏ‰ = 1. Write Ï‰ = Î± + iÎ², where Î±, Î² âˆˆR. Then
eÎ±(cos Î² + i sin Î²) = 1.
Taking modulus, we conclude that eÎ± = 1, so that Î± = 0. It then follows that cos Î² + i sin Î² = 1.
Equating real and imaginary parts, we conclude that cos Î² = 1 and sin Î² = 0, so that Î² = 2Ï€k, where
k âˆˆZ. The second assertion follows. âƒ
Consider now the mapping w = ez. By (5), we have w = ex(cos y + i sin y), where x, y âˆˆR. It
follows that

real axis. The upper edge of the cut, corresponding to arg w = Ï€, is regarded as part of the cut w-plane.
The lower edge of the cut, corresponding to arg w = âˆ’Ï€, is not regarded as part of the cut w-plane.

Chapter 1
COMPLEX NUMBERS
1.1.
Arithmetic and Conjugates
The purpose of this chapter is to give a review of various properties of the complex numbers that we shall
need in the discussion of complex analysis. As the reader is expected to be familiar with the material,
all proofs have been omitted.
The equation x2 +1 = 0 has no solution x âˆˆR. To â€œsolveâ€ this equation, we have to introduce extra
numbers into our number system. To do this, we deï¬ne the number i by i2 + 1 = 0, and then extend the
ï¬eld of all real numbers by adjoining the number i, which is then combined with the real numbers by the
operations addition and multiplication in accordance with the Field axioms of the real number system.
The numbers a + ib, where a, b âˆˆR, of the extended ï¬eld are then added and multiplied in accordance
with the Field axioms, suitably extended, and the restriction i2 + 1 = 0. Note that the number a + 0i,
where a âˆˆR, behaves like the real number a.
What we have said in the last paragraph basically amounts to the following. Consider two complex
numbers a + ib and c + id, where a, b, c, d âˆˆR. We have the addition and multiplication rules
(a + ib) + (c + id) = (a + c) + i(b + d)
and
(a + ib)(c + id) = (ac âˆ’bd) + i(ad + bc).
These lead to the subtraction rule
(a + ib) âˆ’(c + id) = (a âˆ’c) + i(b âˆ’d),
and the division rule, that if c + id Ì¸= 0, then
a + ib
c + id = ac + bd
c2 + d2 + ibc âˆ’ad
c2 + d2 .

1â€“2
W W L Chen : Introduction to Complex Analysis
Note the special case a = 1 and b = 0.
Suppose that z = x+iy, where x, y âˆˆR. The real number x is called the real part of z, and denoted
by x = Rez. The real number y is called the imaginary part of z, and denoted by y = Imz. The set
C = {z = x + iy : x, y âˆˆR} is called the set of all complex numbers. The complex number z = x âˆ’iy is
called the conjugate of z.
It is easy to see that for every z âˆˆC, we have
Rez = z + z
2
and
Imz = z âˆ’z
2i
.
Furthermore, if w âˆˆC, then
z + w = z + w
and
zw = z w.

2.1.
Three Approaches
We start by remarking that analysis is sometimes known as the study of the four Câ€™s: convergence,
continuity, compactness and connectedness. In real analysis, we have studied convergence and continuity
to some depth, but the other two concepts have been somewhat disguised. In this course, we shall try
to illustrate these two latter concepts a little bit more, particularly connectedness.
Complex analysis is the study of complex valued functions of complex variables. Here we shall
restrict the number of variables to one, and study complex valued functions of one complex variable.
Unless otherwise stated, all functions in these notes are of the form f : S â†’C, where S is a set in C.
We shall study the behaviour of such functions using three diï¬€erent approaches. The ï¬rst of these,
discussed in Chapter 3 and usually attributed to Riemann, is based on diï¬€erentiation and involves pairs
of partial diï¬€erential equations called the Cauchy-Riemann equations. The second approach, discussed in
Chapters 4â€“11 and usually attributed to Cauchy, is based on integration and depends on a fundamental
theorem known nowadays as Cauchyâ€™s integral theorem. The third approach, discussed in Chapter 16
and usually attributed to Weierstrass, is based on the theory of power series.
2.2.

We shall study functions of the form f : S â†’C, where S is a set in C. In most situations, various
properties of the point sets S play a crucial role in our study. We therefore begin by discussing various
types of point sets in the complex plane.
Before making any deï¬nitions, let us consider a few examples of sets which frequently occur in our
subsequent discussion.

Definition.

(2)

Definition.
A region is a domain together with all, some or none of its boundary points. A region
which contains all its boundary points is said to be closed. For any region S, we denote by S the closed
region containing S and all its boundary points, and call S the closure of S.
Remark.

fact, our deï¬nition of closedness for a region here is a special case of a much more complicated deï¬nition
of closedness which applies to all point sets.
Definition.

|z| â‰¤M for every z âˆˆS. A region that is closed and bounded is said to be compact.

2.3.
Complex Functions
In these lectures, we study complex valued functions of one complex variable. In other words, we study
functions of the form f : S â†’C, where S is a set in C. Occasionally, we will abuse notation and simply
refer to a function by its formula, without explicitly deï¬ning the domain S. For instance, when we
discuss the function f(z) = 1/z, we implicitly choose a set S which will not include the point z = 0
where the function is not deï¬ned. Also, we may occasionally wish to include the point z = âˆin the
domain or codomain.
We may separate the independent variable z as well as the dependent variable w = f(z) into real
and imaginary parts. Our usual notation will be to write z = x + iy and w = f(z) = u + iv, where
x, y, u, v âˆˆR. It follows that u = u(x, y) and v = v(x, y) can be interpreted as real valued functions of
the two real variables x and y.
Example 2.3.1.
Consider the function f : S â†’C, given by f(z) = z2 and where S = {z âˆˆC : |z| < 2}
is the open disc with radius 2 and centre 0. Using polar coordinates, it is easy to see that the range of
the function is the open disc f(S) = {w âˆˆC : |w| < 4} with radius 4 and centre 0.
Example 2.3.2.
Consider the function f : H â†’C, where H = {z = x + iy âˆˆC : y > 0} is the upper
half-plane and f(z) = z2. Using polar coordinates, it is easy to see that the range of the function is the
complex plane minus the non-negative real axis.

2.4.
Extended Complex Plane
It is sometimes useful to extend the complex plane C by the introduction of the point âˆat inï¬nity. Its
connection with ï¬nite complex numbers can be established by setting z + âˆ= âˆ+ z = âˆfor all z âˆˆC,
and setting z Â· âˆ= âˆÂ· z = âˆfor all non-zero z âˆˆC. We can also write âˆÂ· âˆ= âˆ.
Note that it is not possible to deï¬ne âˆ+ âˆand 0 Â· âˆwithout violating the laws of arithmetic.
However, by special convention, we shall write z/0 = âˆfor z Ì¸= 0 and z/âˆ= 0 for z Ì¸= âˆ.
In the complex plane C, there is no room for a point corresponding to âˆ. We can, of course,
introduce an â€œidealâ€ point which we call the point at inï¬nity. The points in C, together with the point
at inï¬nity, form the extended complex plane. We decree that every straight line on the complex plane
shall pass through the point at inï¬nity, and that no half-plane shall contain the ideal point.
The main purpose of this section is to introduce a geometric model in which each point of the
extended complex plane has a concrete representative. To do this, we shall use the idea of stereographic
projection.
Consider a sphere of radius 1 in R3. A typical point on this sphere will be denoted by P(x1, x2, x3).
Note that x2
1 + x2
2 + x2
3 = 1. Let us call the point N(0, 0, 1) the north pole. The equator of this sphere is
the set of all points of the form (x1, x2, 0), where x2
1 + x2
2 = 1. Consider next the complex plane C. This
can be viewed as a plane in R3. Let us position this plane in such a way that the equator of the sphere
lies on this plane; in other words, our copy of the complex plane is â€œhorizontalâ€ and passes through the
origin. We can further insist that the x-direction on our complex plane is the same as the x1-direction
in R3, and that the y-direction on our complex plane is the same as the x2-direction in R3. Clearly a
typical point z = x + iy on our complex plane C can be identiï¬ed with the point Z(x, y, 0) in R3.

Chapter 2 : Foundations of Complex Analysis
2â€“7
Suppose that Z(x, y, 0) is on the plane. Consider the straight line that passes through Z and the
north pole N. It is not too diï¬ƒcult to see that this straight line intersects the surface of the sphere at
precisely one other point P(x1, x2, x3). In fact, if Z is on the equator of the sphere, then P = Z. If Z is
on the part of the plane outside the sphere, then P is on the northern hemisphere, but is not the north
pole N. If Z is on the part of the plane inside the sphere, then P is on the southern hemisphere. Check
that for Z(0, 0, 0), the point P(0, 0, âˆ’1) is the south pole.
On the other hand, if P is any point on the sphere diï¬€erent from the north pole N, then a straight
line passing through P and N intersects the plane at precisely one point Z. It follows that there is a
pairing of all the points P on the sphere diï¬€erent from the north pole N and all the points on the plane.
This pairing is governed by the requirement that the straight line through any pair must pass through
the north pole N.
We can now visualize the north pole N as the point on the sphere corresponding to the point at
inï¬nity of the plane. The sphere is called the Riemann sphere.
2.5.
Limits and Continuity
The concept of a limit in complex analysis is exactly the same as in real analysis. So, for example, we
say that f(z) â†’L as z â†’z0, or
lim
zâ†’z0 f(z) = L,
if, given any Ïµ > 0, there exists Î´ > 0 such that |f(z) âˆ’L| < Ïµ whenever 0 < |z âˆ’z0| < Î´.
This deï¬nition will be perfectly in order if the function f is deï¬ned in some open set containing
z0, with the possible exception of z0 itself. It follows that if z0 is an interior point of the region S of
deï¬nition of the function, our deï¬nition is in order. However, if z0 is a boundary point of the region S
of deï¬nition of the function, then we agree that the conclusion |f(z) âˆ’L| < Ïµ need only hold for those
z âˆˆS satisfying 0 < |z âˆ’z0| < Î´.
Similarly, we say that a function f(z) is continuous at z0 if f(z) â†’f(z0) as z â†’z0. A similar
qualiï¬cation on z applies if z0 is a boundary point of the region S of deï¬nition of the function. We also
say that a function is continuous in a region if it is continuous at every point of the region.

2â€“8
W W L Chen : Introduction to Complex Analysis
Note that for a function to be continuous in a region, it is enough to have continuity at every point of
the region. Hence the choice of Î´ may depend on a point z0 in question. If Î´ can be chosen independently
of z0, then we have some uniformity as well. To be precise, we make the following deï¬nition.
Definition.
A function f(z) is said to be uniformly continuous in a region S if, given any Ïµ > 0, there
exists Î´ > 0 such that |f(z1) âˆ’f(z2)| < Ïµ for every z1, z2 âˆˆS satisfying |z1 âˆ’z2| < Î´.
Remark.
Note that if we ï¬x z2 to be a point z0 and write z for z1, then we require |f(z) âˆ’f(z0)| < Ïµ
for every z âˆˆS satisfying |z âˆ’z0| < Î´. In other words, Î´ cannot depend on z0.

3.3.
Analytic Functions
In the previous section, we have shown that diï¬€erentiability in complex analysis leads to a pair of partial
diï¬€erential equations. Now partial diï¬€erential equations are seldom of interest at a single point, but
rather in a region. It therefore seems reasonable to make the following deï¬nition.
Definition.
A function f is said to be analytic at a point z0 âˆˆC if it is diï¬€erentiable at every z in
some Ïµ-neighbourhood of the point z0. The function f is said to be analytic in a region if it is analytic
at every point in the region. The function f is said to be entire if it is analytic in C.
Example 3.3.1.
Consider the function f(z) = |z|2. In our usual notation, we clearly have
u = x2 + y2
and
v = 0.
The Cauchy-Riemann equations
2x = 0
and
2y = 0
can only be satisï¬ed at z = 0. It follows that the function is diï¬€erentiable only at the point z = 0, and
is therefore analytic nowhere.
Example 3.3.2.
The function f(z) = z2 is entire.
Example 3.3.3.
Suppose that the function f is analytic in a domain D. Suppose further that f has
constant real part u. Then clearly
âˆ‚u
âˆ‚x = 0
and
âˆ‚u
âˆ‚y = 0.
Since f is analytic in D, it is diï¬€erentiable at every point in D, and so the Cauchy-Riemann equations
hold in D. It follows that
âˆ‚v
âˆ‚x = 0
and
âˆ‚v
âˆ‚y = 0.
Hence f must have constant imaginary part v, and so f must be constant in D.
Example 3.3.4.
Suppose that the function f is analytic in a domain D. Suppose further that f has
constant imaginary part v. A similar argument shows that f must have constant real part u. Hence f
must be constant in D.
Example 3.3.5.

constant modulus. In other words, u2 + v2 = C for some non-negative real number C. Diï¬€erentiating

In this section, we shall generalize various functions that we have studied in real analysis to the complex

Consider ï¬rst of all the exponential function.
It seems reasonable to extend the property

3.5.
Periodicity and its Consequences
One of the fundamental diï¬€erences between real and complex analysis is that the exponential function
is periodic in C.
Definition.
A function f is periodic in C if there is some ï¬xed non-zero Ï‰ âˆˆC such that the identity
f(z + Ï‰) = f(z) holds for every z âˆˆC. Any constant Ï‰ âˆˆC with this property is called a period of f.
THEOREM 3C.
The exponential function ez is periodic in C with period 2Ï€i. Furthermore, any
period Ï‰ âˆˆC of ez is of the form Ï‰ = 2Ï€ki, where k âˆˆZ is non-zero.
Proof.
The ï¬rst assertion follows easily from the observation
e2Ï€i = cos 2Ï€ + i sin 2Ï€ = 1.

eÎ±(cos Î² + i sin Î²) = 1.
Taking modulus, we conclude that eÎ± = 1, so that Î± = 0. It then follows that cos Î² + i sin Î² = 1.
Equating real and imaginary parts, we conclude that cos Î² = 1 and sin Î² = 0, so that Î² = 2Ï€k, where
k âˆˆZ. The second assertion follows. âƒ

The lower edge of the cut, corresponding to arg w = âˆ’Ï€, is not regarded as part of the cut w-plane.

4.1.
Curves in the Complex Plane
Integration of functions of a complex variable is carried out over curves in C and leads to many important
results useful in pure and applied mathematics. In this section, we give a brief introduction to curves in
C. We are interested in complex valued functions of the form
Ï†(t) = Ï†1(t) + iÏ†2(t),
where the functions Ï†1 and Ï†2 are real valued and deï¬ned on some closed interval [A, B] in R. The
functions Ï†1 and Ï†2 are called the real and imaginary parts of the function Ï† respectively.
Example 4.1.1.
The function Ï†(t) = eit, deï¬ned on the interval [0, Ï€], represents the upper half of a
circle centred at the origin and of radius 1. As t varies from 0 to Ï€, Ï†(t) follows this half-circle in an
anticlockwise direction. Also Ï†1(t) = cos t and Ï†2(t) = sin t.

arithmetic of limits, as applied to continuity, holds.

Definition.
A complex valued function Î¶ : [A, B] â†’C is called a curve. The curve is said to be
continuous if Î¶ is continuous in [A, B], and diï¬€erentiable if Î¶ is also diï¬€erentiable in [A, B]. The set
Î¶([A, B]) is called the trace of the curve. The point Î¶(A) is called the initial point of the curve, and the
point Î¶(B) is called the terminal point of the curve.

5.1.
A Restricted Case
Cauchyâ€™s integral theorem states that in a simply connected domain, the integral of an analytic function
over a closed contour is zero. The proof of this general result is rather involved. Here we ï¬rst study a
special case of the theorem in order to develop the basic properties of analytic functions.

5.2.
Analytic Functions in a Star Domain
In this section, we shall use Theorem 5A to establish the existence of an indeï¬nite integral and the
Cauchy integral theorem for analytic functions in a certain class of domains.
Definition.
A domain D âŠ†C is called a star domain if there exists a point z0 âˆˆD such that for every
point z âˆˆD, the line segment joining z and z0 also lies in D. In this case, the point z0 is called a star
centre of the domain D.
Example 5.2.1.
The disc {z : |z| < 1} is a star domain. Every point in this domain is a star centre.
Example 5.2.2.
The complex plane C is a star domain. Again, every point in this domain is a star
centre.
Example 5.2.3.
The complex plane C with the non-negative real axis {x + iy : x â‰¥0, y = 0} deleted
is a star domain. Every point on the remaining part of the real axis is a star centre.
Example 5.2.4.
The set {x + iy : |xy| < 1} is a star domain. The point 0 is the only star centre.
Example 5.2.5.
The interior of the set shown below is a star domain, with a star centre z0 as shown.
THEOREM 5B.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in a star domain D. Then there exists a
function F, analytic in D and such that F â€²(z) = f(z) for every z âˆˆD.

5.3.
Nested Triangles
In this section, we shall give a second proof of Theorem 5A, without the additional assumption that
the derivative f â€²(z) is continuous in D. This proof is based on the following well-known result in real
analysis: Suppose that
a1 â‰¤a2 â‰¤a3 â‰¤. . .
and
b1 â‰¥b2 â‰¥b3 â‰¥. . . .
Suppose further that ak â‰¤bk for every k âˆˆN, and that bk âˆ’ak â†’0 as k â†’âˆ. Then there exists a
unique number â„“âˆˆR such that ak â†’â„“and bk â†’â„“as k â†’âˆ. This is a special case of the Cantor
intersection theorem. In other words, if the intervals
[a1, b1] âŠ‡[a2, b2] âŠ‡[a3, b3] âŠ‡. . .
are nested, so that each contains all subsequent ones, and if their lengths decrease to 0, then the intervals
collapse to a unique point.
We shall now prove Theorem 5A by the method of bisection.

6.1.
Introduction
In this chapter, we study a remarkable formula due to Cauchy and which shows that the values of an
analytic function at the interior points of a disc are determined by the values of the function on the
boundary of the disc.

6.2.
Derivatives
An important consequence of Cauchyâ€™s integral formula is that we can show that an analytic function
possesses derivatives of all orders.
This is a rather remarkable result, and much nicer than in real
analysis. We shall establish this result by a number of steps.
THEOREM 6B.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in a domain D. Then the derivative f â€² is
analytic in D.
Remark.
Recall that in our ï¬rst proof of Theorem 5A, we use the additional assumption that f â€² is
continuous in D. In view of Theorem 6B, it appears that this extra assumption is superï¬‚uous. However,
our proof of Theorem 6B below will depend on Theorem 6A, whose proof uses Theorem 5D, which
follows somewhat from Theorem 5A. Hence we cannot reasonably use our ï¬rst proof of Theorem 5A
without running into the danger of a â€œcircular argumentâ€ of deducing two results from each other and
possibly establishing neither. Note, however, that our second proof of Theorem 5A in Section 5.3 saves
us from this dubious distinction.
THEOREM 6C.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in a domain D. Then the derivative f (n)
exists for every n âˆˆN and is analytic in D.

Example 6.3.1.
We can now prove the Fundamental theorem of algebra, that every non-constant
polynomial P(z) has at least one root. It is easily checked that every such non-constant polynomial
satisï¬es |P(z)| â†’âˆas |z| â†’âˆ. Hence the function 1/P(z) is bounded outside some circle {z : |z| = r}.

bounded in the closed set {z : |z| â‰¤r}. It follows that it is bounded in C. By Liouvilleâ€™s theorem, it
must be constant, a contradiction.

TAYLOR SERIES, UNIQUENESS

7.1.
Remarks on Series
The purpose of this chapter is to show that every analytic function can be represented by a Taylor series,
and to use the Taylor series to study further properties of such functions.
Here we do not propose to have a systematic study of series.
Such a study is postponed until
Chapter 16. In this section, we shall make a brief review of standard terminology.
We are concerned with power series of the form
(1)
a0 + a1(z âˆ’z0) + a2(z âˆ’z0)2 + . . . ,
where a0, a1, a2, . . . âˆˆC and z0 âˆˆC are ï¬xed, and where z belongs to some region in the complex plane
C. For every N âˆˆN, the N-th partial sum of the series (1) is deï¬ned by
(2)
sN(z) = a0 + a1(z âˆ’z0) + a2(z âˆ’z0)2 + . . . + aN(z âˆ’z0)N.
We are interested in the sequence sN(z) of partial sums.
Suppose that a function f(z) and a sequence of functions sN(z) are deï¬ned in a region G in the
complex plane C. The sequence sN(z) is said to converge uniformly to f(z) in G if, given any Ïµ > 0,
there exists N0 = N0(Ïµ) such that
|sN(z) âˆ’f(z)| < Ïµ

for every N > N0 and every z âˆˆG. Note here that the notion of uniformity implies the independence of
N0 from the choice of z in G. We also write

uniformly in G.

7.3.
Uniqueness
Recall the Cauchy integral formula as given by Theorem 6A. To determine the value of an analytic
function at interior points of a disc, we need the values of the function on the boundary of the disc.
On the other hand, if we know the values of f(z0), f â€²(z0), f â€²â€²(z0), . . . of an analytic function f at a
point z0 in a domain D, then the Taylor series determines f(z) in some disc {z : |z âˆ’z0| < R} centred at
z0. It follows that if f(z) is known in some inï¬nitely diï¬€erentiable short arc in the disc {z : |zâˆ’z0| < R},

z1
Îµ1
zâ€™
zâ€™-z1
7â€“6
W W L Chen : Introduction to Complex Analysis
then f(z) is uniquely determined in the disc {z : |z âˆ’z0| < R}, since the derivatives of f(z) can be
calculated by diï¬€erentiation of f(z) on this arc.
The purpose of this section is to extend this second observation to show that an analytic function
f is uniquely determined in a domain D, and not just a disc centred at z0, by the values of f (n)(z0) at
some z0 in D. We shall also show that an analytic function f is determined in a domain D by the values
on a short continuous curve C in D.
THEOREM 7E.
Suppose that two functions f and g are analytic in a domain D. Suppose further
that z0 âˆˆD, and that f(z) = g(z) in some Ïµ-neighbourhood of z0. Then f(z) = g(z) for every z âˆˆD.
Remark.
The proof is based on the following argument: A domain D is open and connected, and
therefore cannot be written as a disjoint union of two non-empty open sets.
Proof of Theorem 7E.

in D. Let

zâ€² âˆˆS1 whenever |zâ€² âˆ’z| < Ïµ1. It follows that {z : |z âˆ’z1| < Ïµ1} âŠ†S1, so that S1 is open.
Suppose next that z2 âˆˆS2. Since z2 âˆˆD, there exists R > 0 such that the disc {z : |z âˆ’z2| < R}

THEOREM 7F.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in the domain D. Suppose further that z0 âˆˆD,
and that f(z0) = 0. Then either f(z) is identically zero in D or else there exists n âˆˆN such that
f(z) = (z âˆ’z0)ng(z),
where the function g is analytic in D, and
g(z0) = f (n)(z0)
n!
Ì¸= 0.
Definition.
If the latter conclusion of Theorem 7F holds, then we say that the function has a zero of
order n at z0. Furthermore, if n = 1, then we say that the function f has a simple zero at z0.
THEOREM 7G.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in the domain D. Suppose further that f(z)
is not identically zero in D. Then for every z0 âˆˆD such that f(z0) = 0, there exists Ïµ > 0 such that
f(z) Ì¸= 0 for every 0 < |z âˆ’z0| < Ïµ. In other words, the zeros of f are isolated.
Proof of Theorem 7F.
Clearly there exists R > 0 such that the disc {z : |z âˆ’z0| < R} is contained
in D. Suppose that f (n)(z0) = 0 for every n âˆˆN. Then it follows from Theorem 7A that f(z) is

We complete this section by proving the following result, which shows that an analytic function is
determined in a domain D by the values on a short continuous curve in D.
THEOREM 7H.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in the domain D. Suppose further that zn
is a sequence of distinct points having a limit z0 âˆˆD, and that f(zn) = g(zn) for every n âˆˆN. Then
f(z) = g(z) for every z âˆˆD.
Proof.
For every z âˆˆD, write h(z) = f(z)âˆ’g(z). Then the function h is analytic in D. Furthermore,
it follows from continuity that h(z0) = 0. Since h(zn) = 0 for every n âˆˆN and zn â†’z0 as n â†’âˆ, the
zero z0 of the function h is not isolated. It follows from Theorem 7G that h(z) is identically zero in D.

The following alternative form of the Maximum principle is perhaps more useful.
THEOREM 7K.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in a bounded domain D, and continuous in
the closed region D. Then |f(z)| attains its maximum on the boundary of D.
Proof.
It is well known from real analysis that |f(z)| assumes its maximum somewhere in the closed
bounded region D. By Theorem 7J, this maximum cannot be attained in D, and so must be attained
on the boundary of D. âƒ

8.1.
Removable Singularities
Suppose that a function f is analytic in the punctured disc {z : 0 < |z âˆ’z0| < R}. Observe that it is not
necessary for f to be deï¬ned at the point z0. We say that the function f has an isolated singularity at z0.
Our purpose is to show that there are only three possible ways in which f(z) can behave in a punctured
neighbourhood of z0. To illustrate the ï¬rst of these, let us ï¬rst consider the following examples.
Example 8.1.1.

then f is now analytic at z = 0, and we have removed the isolated singularity.

8.2.
Poles
Definition.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in the punctured disc {z : 0 < |z âˆ’z0| < R}.
Suppose further that
(11)
f(z) =

that f has a pole of order n at z0. Furthermore, if n = 1, then we say that f has a simple pole at z0.
THEOREM 8B.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in the punctured disc {z : 0 < |z âˆ’z0| < R}.

Remark.
Note that a function f has a pole of order n at z0 if and only if the function 1/f has a zero
of order n at z0.
8.3.
Essential Singularities
Definition.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in the punctured disc {z : 0 < |z âˆ’z0| < R}.
Suppose further that the isolated singularity at z0 is neither removable nor a pole. Then we say that f
has an essential singularity at z0.
Example 8.3.1.
The function e1/z is analytic at every z Ì¸= 0. It has an isolated singularity at z = 0.
Let us restrict z to be real numbers, and consider e1/x, where x > 0. Clearly

To illustrate the wild behaviour of an analytic function near an essential singularity, we mention
Picardâ€™s theorem that such a function assumes all values except possibly one in any neighbourhood of
an essential singularity. The following result is somewhat weaker, and shows that such a function comes
arbitrarily close to any given complex number in any neighbourhood of an essential singularity.
THEOREM 8C.
(CASORATI-WEIERSTRASS)
Suppose that a function f is analytic in the punc-
tured disc {z : 0 < |z âˆ’z0| < R}, with an essential singularity at z0. Then given any w âˆˆC and any
real numbers Ïµ > 0 and Î´ > 0, there exists z in the punctured disc satisfying
0 < |z âˆ’z0| < Î´

Proof.

which is removable, in view of Theorem 8A. It follows that by deï¬ning g(z0) appropriately, the function
g is analytic in the disc {z : |z âˆ’z0| < Î´}. On the other hand, the function g is clearly not identically
zero in {z : |z âˆ’z0| < Î´}. Furthermore, note that

If g(z0) Ì¸= 0, then f is analytic at z0. If g(z0) = 0, then f has a pole at z0. In either case, the conclusion
contradicts the assumption that f has an essential singularity at z0, and this completes the proof. âƒ

THEOREM 8F.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in the punctured disc {z : 0 < |z âˆ’z0| < R},
with an isolated singularity at z0. Suppose further that the Laurent coeï¬ƒcients an are given by (15).
(a) The function f either is analytic or has a removable singularity at z0 if and only if an = 0 for every
n < 0.
(b) The function f has a pole at z0 if and only if a positive but ï¬nite number of coeï¬ƒcients an with
n < 0 are non-zero.
(c) The function f has an essential singularity at z0 if and only if an inï¬nite number of coeï¬ƒcients an
with n < 0 are non-zero.

Proof.

series, and that a Laurent series with no principal part is a Taylor series. To prove part (b), note ï¬rst
of all that if a positive but ï¬nite number of coeï¬ƒcients an with n < 0 are non-zero, then there exists
m > 0 such that aâˆ’m Ì¸= 0 but an = 0 for every n < âˆ’m. In this case, we have

shows that f has a pole of order m at z0. The converse is given in Example 8.6.1. Part (b) follows. Part
(c) follows immediately from (a) and (b). âƒ

We conclude this chapter by making a remark on various equivalent deï¬nitions of analyticity in a
domain D. The reader is advised to check the following theorem very carefully.
THEOREM 8G.
For any function f and any domain D, the following statements are equivalent:
(a) f(z) is analytic in D.
(b) f(z) has continuous derivatives of all orders in D.
(c) f â€²(z) exists and is continuous in D.
(d) f â€²(z) exists in D.
(e) f â€²(z) exists in D except possibly at a ï¬nite number of points in D, and f(z) is continuous at these
exceptional points.
(f) f(z) can be represented uniformly by its Taylor series in the neighbourhood of every point in D.

CAUCHYâ€™S INTEGRAL THEOREM REVISITED
9.1.
Simply Connected Domains
Cauchyâ€™s integral theorem states that in a simply connected domain, the integral of an analytic function
over a closed contour is zero. In Chapter 5, we have studied a special case of the theorem in order to
develop the basic properties of analytic functions. More precisely, we have studied the special case when
the domain in question is a star domain. For most purposes in pure and applied mathematics, this
special case is adequate. However, by introducing further line segments, one may be able to extend the
results to curves which may not lie in star domains.
Example 9.1.1.
It is easy to see that the domain D = {z : 2 < |z| < 6 and Imz > 0} is the part of
the annulus {z : 2 < |z| < 6} in the upper half plane. It is not diï¬ƒcult to check that D is not a star
domain.
Let
D1 = {z : 2 < |z| < 6 and Imz > 0 and Rez < 1}
and
D2 = {z : 2 < |z| < 6 and Imz > 0 and Rez > âˆ’1}.

W W L Chen : Introduction to Complex Analysis

âˆ’2 + 5i and 2 + 5i respectively.
If C is a simple closed contour lying in D, then by introducing line segments along the imaginary axis,
it is not diï¬ƒcult to see that there is a simple closed contour C1 in D1 and a simple closed contour C2 in

Note now that we can apply Theorem 5D to the two integrals on the right hand side.
However, it is of great theoretical interest to formulate results that are less restricting. Here we
introduce the idea of a simply connected domain. This can be done in a number of ways. Here we use
the Jordan curve theorem for simple closed polygons.
Definition.
By a polygonal curve, we mean a curve Î¶ : [A, B] â†’C which is continuous and piecewise
linear. In other words, there exists a dissection
A = A1 < B1 = A2 < B2 = . . . = Ak < Bk = B
such that for every j = 1, . . . , k, the edge Î¶ : [Aj, Bj] â†’C is of the form Î¶(t) = Î±jt+Î²j, where Î±j, Î²j âˆˆC
and we assume further that Î±j Ì¸= 0.
Definition.
By a simple closed polygon, we mean a polygonal curve that is closed and does not
intersect itself; in other words, if Î¶(t1) Ì¸= Î¶(t2) whenever t1 Ì¸= t2, with the one exception that Î¶(A) = Î¶(B).

simply connected

Chapter 9 : Cauchyâ€™s Integral Theorem Revisited
9â€“3
It is not hard to prove the Jordan curve theorem for a simple closed polygon, that such a polygon
divides the plane C into two domains, the bounded one interior to the polygon and the unbounded one
exterior to the polygon. Here we shall not give the proof.
Definition.
A domain D is said to be simply connected if the interior of every simple closed polygon
in D is contained in D.
Remark.
Recall that a domain is an open connected set. A simply connected domain is one which is
free of holes or cuts in its interior.
9.2.
Cauchyâ€™s Integral Theorem
In this section, we indicate the proof of the following generalization of Theorem 5B.
THEOREM 9A.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in a simply connected domain D. Then there
exists a function F, analytic in D and such that F â€²(z) = f(z) for every z âˆˆD.
In view of Remark (1) immediately after Theorem 4A, Theorem 9A immediately leads to the
following generalization of Theorem 5D.

further that C is a closed contour lying in D. Then

10.1.
Cauchyâ€™s Residue Theorem
If we extend Cauchyâ€™s integral theorem to functions having isolated singularities, then the integral is in
general not equal to zero. Instead, each singularity contributes a term called the residue. Our principal
aim in this section is to show that this residue depends only on the coeï¬ƒcient of (zâˆ’z0)âˆ’1 in the Laurent
expansion of the function near the singularity z0, since all the other powers of z âˆ’z0 has single valued
integrals and so integrate to zero.

10.3.
Principle of the Argument
In this section, we shall show that the residue theorem, when applied suitably, can be used to ï¬nd the
number of zeros of an analytic function, as well as the number of zeros minus the number of poles of a
meromorphic function.
The main idea underpinning our discussion can be summarized by the following two results.
THEOREM 10C.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in a neighbourhood of z0. Suppose further
that f has a zero of order m at z0. Then the function f â€²/f is analytic in a punctured neighbourhood of
z0, with a simple pole at z0 with residue m.

pose further that f has a pole of order m at z0. Then the function f â€²/f is analytic in a punctured
neighbourhood of z0, with a simple pole at z0 with residue âˆ’m.

We can write f(z) = (z âˆ’z0)mg(z), where g(z) is analytic in a neighbour-

The main result in this section is the Principle of the argument, as stated below.
THEOREM 10E.
Suppose that a function f is meromorphic in a simply connected domain D. Sup-
pose further that C is a Jordan curve in D, followed in the positive (anticlockwise) direction, and that
f has no zeros or poles on C. If N denotes the number of zeros of f in the interior of C, counted with
multiplicities, and if P denotes the number of poles of f in the interior of C, counted with multiplicities,
then
1
2Ï€i

C
f â€²(z)
f(z) dz = N âˆ’P.
Proof.
Note that by Theorems 10C and 10D, the poles of the function f â€²/f are precisely at the zeros
and poles of f. Furthermore, a zero of f of order m gives rise to a residue m for f â€²/f, so that the residues
of f â€²/f arising from the zeros of f are equal to the number of zeros of f counted with multiplicities, and
this number is N. On the other hand, a pole of f of order m gives rise to a residue âˆ’m for f â€²/f, so
that the residues of f â€²/f arising from the poles of f are equal to minus the number of poles of f counted
with multiplicities, and this number is P. It follows that the sum of the residues is equal to N âˆ’P. The
result now follows from Theorem 10B applied to the function f â€²/f. âƒ

Note that Ref(iy) > 0, so that f(iy) is in the ï¬rst or fourth quadrant of the complex plane. In fact,

so that var(arg f(z), C) must be an integer multiple of 2Ï€. It follows that var(arg f(z), C) = 2Ï€. Note
now that the function f has no poles in the ï¬rst quadrant. It follows from the Argument principle that
f has exactly one zero inside the contour C for all large R. Hence f has exactly one zero in the ï¬rst
quadrant of the complex plane.
To ï¬nd the number of zeros in a region, the following result provides an opportunity to either bypass
the Argument principle or at least enable one to apply the Argument principle to a simpler function.
Needless to say, the proof is based on an application of the Argument principle.
THEOREM 10F.
(ROUCHÂ´Eâ€™S THEOREM)
Suppose that functions f and g are analytic in a sim-
ply connected domain D, and that C is a Jordan contour in D. Suppose further that |f(z)| > |g(z)| on
C. Then f and f + g have the same number of zeros inside C.
We shall give two proofs of this result. The ï¬rst is the one given in most texts.

Chapter 11
EVALUATION OF DEFINITE INTEGRALS
11.1.
Introduction
The calculus of residues often provides an eï¬ƒcient method for evaluating certain real and complex
integrals. This is particularly important when it is not possible to ï¬nd indeï¬nite integrals explicitly.
Even in cases when ordinary methods of calculus can be applied, the use of residues often proves to be
a labour saving device.
Naturally, the calculus of residues gives rise to complex integrals, and this suggests that we may
be at a disadvantage if we want to evaluate real integrals. In practice, this is seldom the case, since a
complex integral is equivalent to two real integrals.
However, there are limitations to this approach. The integrand must be closely associated with some
analytic function. We usually want to integrate some elementary functions, and these can be extended
to the complex domain. Also, the techniques of complex integration applies to closed curves while a real
integral is over an interval. It follows that we need a device to reduce our problem to one which concerns
integration over closed curves. There are a number of ways to achieve this, depending on circumstances.
The technique is best learned by studying typical examples, and complete mastery does not guarantee
success.

12.1.
A Local Property of Analytic Functions
Consider an arc C given by z(t), where t âˆˆ[A, B]. For every t âˆˆ[A, B], we can write
z(t) = x(t) + iy(t),
where x(t), y(t) âˆˆR. Then the vector
zâ€²(t) = xâ€²(t) + iyâ€²(t)
has slope dy/dx, which is also the slope of the arc C. It follows that if zâ€²(t0) Ì¸= 0, then the vector zâ€²(t0)
is tangent to the arc at the point z0 = z(t0), and arg zâ€²(t0) is the angle this directed tangent makes with
the positive x-axis.

W W L Chen : Introduction to Complex Analysis
Suppose now that C lies in a domain D, and that a function f(z) is analytic in D. Consider the
arc f(C) given by w(t) = f(z(t)), where t âˆˆ[A, B]. By the Chain rule,
wâ€²(t) = f â€²(z(t))zâ€²(t).
Suppose now that zâ€²(t0) Ì¸= 0 and f â€²(z0) Ì¸= 0, where z0 = z(t0). Then wâ€²(t0) Ì¸= 0, and
arg wâ€²(t0) = arg f â€²(z0) + arg zâ€²(t0).
We can interpret this geometrically in the following way: The angle between the directed tangent to C
at z0 = z(t0) and the directed tangent to f(C) at f(z0) is arg f â€²(z0).
In other words, under the mapping f, the directed tangent to any arc through z0 is rotated by an
angle arg f â€²(z0), independent of the choice of the arc through z0. This also means that if two arcs C1
and C2 intersect at z0 at an angle, then the two arcs f(C1) and f(C2) intersect at f(z0) at the same
angle.
Definition.
An analytic function f is said to be conformal at z0 if the following condition is satisï¬ed:
If two arcs C1 and C2 meet at z0, then the angle from f(C1) to f(C2) at f(z0) is the same as the angle
from C1 to C2 at z0.
We have in fact proved the following result.
THEOREM 12A.
Suppose that a function f is analytic in a domain D, and that z0 âˆˆD. Suppose
further that f â€²(z0) Ì¸= 0. Then f is conformal at z0.
Remark.
Conformality is considered a local property of analytic functions. Note also that
lim
zâ†’z0
|f(z) âˆ’f(z0)|
|z âˆ’z0|
= |f â€²(z0)|.
This shows that |f â€²(z0)| is a local scaling factor of the function f at z0, and is independent of the
direction of z from z0.
To say that an analytic function is conformal usually means that it is locally one-to-one. In partic-
ular, we have the following result.
THEOREM 12B.
Suppose that a function f is analytic and one-to-one in a domain D. Then f is
conformal at every point in D.
This follows immediately from the result below and Theorem 12A.
THEOREM 12C.
Suppose that a non-constant function f is analytic in a domain D, and that z0 âˆˆD.
Suppose further that f â€²(z0) = 0. Then f cannot be one-to-one in any disc containing z0.

Chapter 12 : Harmonic Functions and Conformal Mappings
12â€“3
Proof.
Write w0 = f(z0). Since f(z) is not identically constant, the function g(z) = f(z) âˆ’w0 is not
identically zero. If f â€²(z0) = 0, then g(z) has a zero of ï¬nite order at least 2 at z0. Since the zeros of an
analytic function are isolated, we can choose r > 0 so small that both g(z) and f â€²(z) have no zeros in
the punctured disc {z : 0 < |z âˆ’z0| â‰¤r}. Then
m = min
zâˆˆC |g(z)| > 0,
where C = {z : |z âˆ’z0| = r} denotes the boundary of the disc. Let w âˆˆC satisfy 0 < |w âˆ’w0| < m.
Then
|w0 âˆ’w| < |g(z)|
on C. It follows from RouchÂ´eâ€™s theorem that the functions g(z) and g(z) + (w0 âˆ’w) have the same
number of zeros inside C. Hence
g(z) + (w0 âˆ’w) = f(z) âˆ’w
has at least two zeros inside C.
Clearly none of these zeros can be z0.
Since f â€²(z) Ì¸= 0 inside the
punctured disc, it follows that these zeros must be simple, and so distinct. âƒ
Example 12.1.1.
The exponential function f(z) = ez has non-zero derivative at every z âˆˆC, and is
therefore conformal at every z âˆˆC. Note, however, that f : C â†’C is not one-to-one. On the other
hand, the function is one-to-one if we restrict its domain of deï¬nition to any strip of the form
{z = x + iy : a â‰¤y â‰¤b},
where 0 < b âˆ’a < 2Ï€. We therefore say that the exponential function is locally one-to-one.
12.2.
Laplaceâ€™s Equation
Recall that a continuous function Ï†(x, y) that satisï¬es Laplaceâ€™s equation
âˆ‚2Ï†
âˆ‚x2 + âˆ‚2Ï†
âˆ‚y2 = 0
in a domain D âŠ†C is said to be harmonic in D. In Theorem 3E, we have shown that such a function
can be written as the real part of an analytic function f in D.
Our main task in this section is to show that a harmonic function can be carried from one domain
to another by analytic functions. More precisely, we prove the following result.
THEOREM 12D.
Suppose that D, Dâ€² âŠ†C are domains, and that f : D â†’Dâ€² is a one-to-one and
onto analytic function. Suppose further that for every z âˆˆD, we write w = f(z), where z = x + iy and
w = u + iv, with x, y, u, v âˆˆR. Then for every function Ï†(x, y) harmonic in D, the function Ïˆ(u, v),
deï¬ned by
(1)
Ïˆ(u, v) = Ï†(x(u, v), y(u, v)),
is harmonic in Dâ€².
Theorem 12D is particularly useful in applications which involve the solution of the Dirichlet problem
concerning the question of ï¬nding a harmonic function in a domain D which takes speciï¬ed values on
the boundary of D. Once we solve this problem for a particular domain, we can use Theorem 12D to ï¬nd

12.3.
Global Properties of Analytic Functions
We begin by studying the following result which can be considered both local and global. It can be
proved by means of RouchÂ´eâ€™s theorem in the same spirit as the proof of Theorem 12C.
THEOREM 12F.
(OPEN MAPPING THEOREM)
Suppose that a non-constant function f is ana-
lytic in a domain D. Then f(S) is open for any open set S âŠ†D. More speciï¬cally, suppose that z0 âˆˆD,
and that w0 = f(z0). Then for all suï¬ƒciently small Ïµ > 0, there exists Î´ > 0 such that
{w : |w âˆ’w0| < Î´} âŠ†f({z : |z âˆ’z0| < Ïµ}).
In other words, w0 is an interior point of f(D).

Proof.
Since f(z) is not identically constant, the function g(z) = f(z) âˆ’w0 is not identically zero,
and has a zero of ï¬nite order at z0. Since the zeros of an analytic function are isolated, we can choose
r < Ïµ so small that g(z) has no zeros in the punctured disc {z : 0 < |z âˆ’z0| â‰¤r}. Then
Î´ = min

Let us examine Theorem 12D again. We have assumed that both D and Dâ€² are domains. The
following result allows us to somewhat relax our assumptions.
THEOREM 12G.
Suppose that a non-constant function f is analytic in a domain D. Then f(D) is
a domain.
Remark.
Recall that an open set S is connected if every two points in S can be joined by the union
of a ï¬nite number of line segments lying in S. An easy theorem in real analysis states that any contour
can be approximated arbitrarily well by the union of a ï¬nite number of line segments. It follows that S
is connected if every two points in S can be joined by a contour lying in S.
Proof of Theorem 12G.
To show that f(D) is a domain, we need to show that it is open and
connected. In view of Theorem 12F, it remains to show that f(D) is connected. Suppose that w1, w2 âˆˆ
f(D). Then there exist z1, z2 âˆˆD such that f(z1) = w1 and f(z2) = w2. Since D is connected, z1 and
z2 can be joined by the union of a ï¬nite number of line segments lying in S. The image of each line
segment under f is an arc in f(D), since f is diï¬€erentiable in D.
It follows that w1 and w2 can be joined by a contour lying in f(D). âƒ
We conclude this section by stating the following result.
THEOREM 12H.
(RIEMANN MAPPING THEOREM)
Suppose that D is a simply connected do-
main in C which is diï¬€erent from C. Then there exists a one-to-one and onto analytic function of the
type f : D â†’U, where U = {w : |w| < 1} denotes the unit open disc.

Chapter 12 : Harmonic Functions and Conformal Mappings
12â€“7
Remarks.
(1)
If we prescribe a point in D and a direction through this point, then there is a unique
function of the type described which maps this point and direction to the origin and the positive x-axis
respectively.
(2)
The proof can be split into three steps. Let z0 âˆˆD be ï¬xed. One begins by showing that
the collection S of one-to-one analytic functions of the type f : D â†’U and satisfying the conditions
f(z0) = 0 and f â€²(z0) > 0 is non-empty. One then shows that there is an extremal member in S with
greatest f â€²(z0). Finally, one shows that if a member in S is not an onto function, then it cannot be this
extremal member. It follows that the extremal member satisï¬es the requirements of the theorem.
(3)
Unfortunately, Theorem 12H is a purely existence theorem, and so cannot be used in conjunc-
tion with Theorem 12D. In Chapters 13â€“14, we shall study some techniques which may enable us to
construct such a function.

We now state a result which includes these four remarks.
THEOREM 13C.
The inversion function f : C â†’C, given by f(z) = 1/z for every non-zero z âˆˆC,
and f(0) = âˆand f(âˆ) = 0, is one-to-one and onto. On the other hand, its inverse function is itself.
Furthermore, the image under this function of a line or a circle in C is also a line or a circle in C.
Remarks.
(1)
We have in fact shown the following: Under the inversion function, the image of a
line through the origin is a line through the origin, the image of a line not through the origin is a circle
through the origin, the image of a circle through the origin is a line not through the origin, and the
image of a circle not through the origin is a circle not through the origin.
(2)
If we think of a line as a circle of inï¬nite radius, then we can think of circles and lines as
belonging to the â€œsameâ€ class. The inversion function therefore maps members of this class to members
of this class.
13.3.
A Generalization
If we extend any linear function discussed in Â§13.1 to a function of the type f : C â†’C by writing
f(âˆ) = âˆ, then it is easy to see that this extended function f : C â†’C is also one-to-one and onto, and
that its inverse function f âˆ’1 : C â†’C is also a linear function.
Note also that the class of all circles and lines in C is carried to itself by all linear functions as well
as the inversion function. We now try to generalize these two types of functions.

THEOREM 13D.
Suppose that T : C â†’C is a MÂ¨obius transformation. Then
(a) T is the composition of a sequence of translations, magniï¬cations, rotations and inversions;

Definition.
A ï¬xed point z âˆˆC of a MÂ¨obius transformation T : C â†’C is a solution of the equation
T(z) = z.
THEOREM 13E.
A MÂ¨obius transformation T : C â†’C has at most two distinct ï¬xed points in C
unless T(z) = z identically.
Proof.
Suppose that
T(z) = az + b
cz + d,
where ad âˆ’bc Ì¸= 0. If c = 0, then ad Ì¸= 0, so that T is a linear function. In this case, the equation
T(z) = z becomes az + b = dz. If T(z) is not identically equal to z, then a Ì¸= d or b Ì¸= 0, so that this
equation has at most one solution in C. Suppose next that c Ì¸= 0. Then clearly âˆis not a ï¬xed point.
The equation T(z) = z is now a quadratic equation, and so has at most two distinct roots in C. âƒ
It follows from Theorem 13E that a MÂ¨obius transformation must be the identity function if it has
three ï¬xed points. Suppose now that S and T are MÂ¨obius transformations, and that there exist distinct
z1, z2, z3 âˆˆC such that S(zj) = T(zj) for j = 1, 2, 3. By Theorem 13D(c)(e), the composition Sâˆ’1 â—¦T is
also a MÂ¨obius transformation. Clearly (Sâˆ’1 â—¦T)(zj) = zj for j = 1, 2, 3, so that Sâˆ’1 â—¦T has three ï¬xed
points, and so must be the identity function. In other words, (Sâˆ’1 â—¦T)(z) = z, and so S(z) = T(z), for
every z âˆˆC. We summarize this observation below.
THEOREM 13F.
Suppose that two MÂ¨obius transformations S : C â†’C and T : C â†’C are equal at
three distinct points in C. Then S(z) = T(z) for every z âˆˆC.
Next, we show that three points determine uniquely a MÂ¨obius transfomation.

Recall that a MÂ¨obius transformation T : C â†’C, given by

circle. Suppose, on the other hand, that a circle or line does not contain the pole z = âˆ’d/c of T, then
its image under T cannot contain the point at âˆ, and is therefore a circle rather than a line.

does not contain the point at âˆ. Since T : C â†’C is one-to-one and onto, and since an analytic function

13.5.
Symmetry and MÂ¨obius Transformations
Definition.
We say that two points z1, z2 âˆˆC are symmetric with respect to a line L if L is the
perpendicular bisector of the line segment joining z1 and z2.
Suppose that z1, z2 âˆˆC are symmetric with respect to a line L. Then it is easy to see that every
circle or line passing through both z1 and z2 intersects L at right angles.
Using this observation, we make the following deï¬nition.
Definition.
We say that two points z1, z2 âˆˆC are symmetric with respect to a circle C if every circle
or line passing through both z1 and z2 intersects C at right angles.

13â€“12
W W L Chen : Introduction to Complex Analysis
Remarks.
(1)
Consider the circle C = {z : |z âˆ’z0| = r} with centre z0 and radius r. Then it can be
shown that two points z1 inside C and z2 outside C are symmetric with respect to C if and only if there
exist Ï, Î¸ âˆˆR satisfying 0 < Ï < r and such that
z1 = z0 + ÏeiÎ¸
and
z2 = z0 + r2
Ï eiÎ¸;
in other words, if and only if (z1 âˆ’z0)(z2 âˆ’z0) = r2.
(2)
We also say that the centre of a circle C and the point at âˆare symmetric with respect to
the circle C.
(3)
Note that a line can be interpreted as a circle of inï¬nite radius. It follows that our deï¬nition
covers symmetry with respect to both lines and circles.
THEOREM 13H.
(SYMMETRY PRINCIPLE)
Suppose that T : C â†’C is a MÂ¨obius transforma-
tion. Suppose further that C is a circle or line in C. Then two points z1, z2 âˆˆC are symmetric with
respect to C if and only if T(z1) and T(z2) are symmetric with respect to T(C).
Proof.
Note that T maps the class of lines and circles in C to itself. Note also that T is conformal at
all points where it is analytic, and so preserves orthogonality. âƒ

Definition.
A function f(z) of the form (2) is called a Schwarz-Christoï¬€el transformation.
14.3.
Polygons
Note that the function (2) maps the real axis onto a polygonal path.
We now wish to construct a
one-to-one analytic function that maps the upper half plane H onto the interior of a given polygon P.
The idea is to tailor a Schwarz-Christoï¬€el transformation to achieve this.
Suppose that the vertices of the polygon P are given by w1, . . . , wk in the anticlockwise direction.
Let us follow the edges of the polygon P. At vertex wj, suppose that we make a right turn of angle Î¸jÏ€,
where âˆ’1 < Î¸j < 1, with the convention that Î¸j < 0 denotes a left turn.

Since P is a polygon and its vertices are given in the anticlockwise direction, we must have
Î¸1Ï€ + . . . + Î¸kÏ€ = âˆ’2Ï€.
It is an elementary fact in geometry that if we know the vertices w1, . . . , wkâˆ’1 and angles Î¸1Ï€, . . . , Î¸kâˆ’1Ï€
of the polygon P, then the last vertex wk and angle Î¸kÏ€ are uniquely determined. The idea is therefore

14â€“4
W W L Chen : Introduction to Complex Analysis
to ï¬nd real numbers x1 < . . . < xkâˆ’1 to act as preimages of the vertices w1, . . . , wkâˆ’1, and to assume
that x = âˆis the preimage of the vertex wk.
Suppose that x1 < . . . < xkâˆ’1. Clearly the function
g(z) =

[z0,z]
(Î¶ âˆ’x1)Î¸1 . . . (Î¶ âˆ’xkâˆ’1)Î¸kâˆ’1 dÎ¶
maps the real line onto some polygon Q of k sides. However, the polygon Q may not be the polygon
P, but at least it has the required right hand turn angles Î¸1, . . . , Î¸kâˆ’1 at the vertices g(x1), . . . , g(xkâˆ’1).
We can adjust the lengths of the sides of the polygon Q by choosing x1, . . . , xkâˆ’1 carefully, so that Q is
similar to the polygon P. Once this is achieved, we can then map the polygon Q to the polygon P by a
linear transformation.
We state, without proof, the following important result.
THEOREM 14A.
Suppose that P is a polygon with vertices w1, . . . , wk in the anticlockwise direction,
with corresponding right turns of angles Î¸1Ï€, . . . , Î¸kÏ€ respectively, where âˆ’1 < Î¸1, . . . , Î¸k < 1. Then there
exists a function of the form
f(z) = A

[z0,z]
(Î¶ âˆ’x1)Î¸1 . . . (Î¶ âˆ’xkâˆ’1)Î¸kâˆ’1 dÎ¶ + B,
where A, B âˆˆC, that maps the upper half plane H one-to-one and conformally onto the interior of P,
with
f(x1) = w1,
. . . ,
f(xkâˆ’1) = wkâˆ’1,
f(âˆ) = wk.
Remarks.
(1)
Note that we do not even need to have very precise information on wk and Î¸k.
(2)
Certain inï¬nite regions can sometimes be thought of as inï¬nite polygons. In this case, it is
sometimes convenient to take wk as the point at inï¬nity, as we need no information on the angle Î¸k
when we use Theorem 14A.
(3)
It can be shown that a Schwarz-Christoï¬€el transformation can be uniquely determined by three
points, as is the case for MÂ¨obius transformations. This can be interpreted as three degrees of freedom
in our construction of the transformation. One of these is used by taking f(âˆ) = wk. We can therefore
aï¬€ord to choose x1 and x2 freely, subject to the restriction that âˆ’âˆ< x1 < x2 < âˆ.
(4)
Occasionally, we may choose extra points apart from x1 and x2 due to symmetry properties
of the polygon P. We shall illustrate this point in Examples 14.4.3â€“14.4.5 below.
(5)
Note that the integrals involved may be impossible to calculate in practice. Numerical tech-
niques are often used. However, we shall not discuss these here.

in the original region. Here we choose a branch of the square root that is positive for large positive w.

UNIFORM CONVERGENCE
16.1.
Uniform Convergence of Sequences
Recall that if a sequence an of complex numbers converges to a, then, given any Ïµ > 0, there exists
N âˆˆR such that |an âˆ’a| < Ïµ whenever n > N.
We can extend this to pointwise convergence in a region D âŠ†C in a natural way. A sequence of
complex valued functions an(z) deï¬ned on D converges pointwise to a function a(z) deï¬ned on D if,
given any Ïµ > 0 and any z âˆˆD, there exists N âˆˆR such that |an(z) âˆ’a(z)| < Ïµ whenever n > N. Here
the value of N may depend on the choice of z âˆˆD. Indeed, for any ï¬xed z âˆˆD, we simply consider the
convergence of the sequence an(z) of complex numbers to the complex number a(z). The region D does
not play any essential part in the argument apart from providing the complex numbers z in question.
In this chapter, we introduce the idea of uniformity to the question of convergence. Put simply,
uniformity transfers the dependence of N on z to dependence of N only on the region D containing the
complex numbers z in question. More precisely, we have the following deï¬nition.
Definition.
Suppose that D âŠ†C is a region. We say that a sequence of complex valued functions
an(z) converges uniformly in D to a function a(z), denoted by an(z) â†’a(z) as n â†’âˆuniformly in D,
if, given any Ïµ > 0, there exists N âˆˆR such that for every z âˆˆD, |an(z) âˆ’a(z)| < Ïµ whenever n > N.
Remark.
Note that N no longer depends on the choice of z âˆˆD. Note also that a precise deï¬nition
can be given by requiring N âˆˆR to satisfy
sup
zâˆˆD
|an(z) âˆ’a(z)| < Ïµ
whenever n > N.

W W L Chen : Introduction to Complex Analysis

|an(z) âˆ’0| = |z|

16.2.
Consequences of Uniform Convergence
In this section, we show that uniform convergence carries a number of properties of the sequence over
to the limit function.
The following three results concern respectively continuity, integrability and
diï¬€erentiability.
THEOREM 16A.
Suppose that for every n âˆˆN, the function an(z) is continuous in a region D âŠ†C.
Suppose further that an(z) â†’a(z) as n â†’âˆuniformly in D. Then a(z) is continuous in D.
Proof.
Suppose that z0 âˆˆD is ï¬xed. For every z âˆˆD, we have
a(z) âˆ’a(z0) = a(z) âˆ’an(z) + an(z) âˆ’an(z0) + an(z0) âˆ’a(z0),
so that
(1)
|a(z) âˆ’a(z0)| â‰¤|an(z) âˆ’a(z)| + |an(z) âˆ’an(z0)| + |an(z0) âˆ’a(z0)|.
Given any Ïµ > 0, there exists N (independent of the choice of z âˆˆD) such that
(2)
|an(z) âˆ’a(z)| < Ïµ
3
and
|an(z0) âˆ’a(z0)| < Ïµ
3
whenever n > N. We now choose any n > N and consider the function an(z). Clearly this function is
continuous at z0. Hence given any Ïµ > 0, there exists Î´ > 0 such that
(3)
|an(z) âˆ’an(z0)| < Ïµ
3
whenever |z âˆ’z0| < Î´.
Combining (1)â€“(3), we conclude that |a(z) âˆ’a(z0)| < Ïµ whenever |z âˆ’z0| < Î´, so that a(z) is continuous
at z0. Since z0 âˆˆD is arbitrary, the result follows. âƒ

16.3.
Cauchy Sequences
Suppose that a sequence of complex numbers an converges to a. Then given any Ïµ > 0, there exists
N âˆˆR such that |an âˆ’a| < Ïµ/2 whenever n > N. It follows that
|an âˆ’am| â‰¤|an âˆ’a| + |am âˆ’a| < Ïµ
whenever m, n > N.
Definition.
We say that a sequence of complex numbers an is a Cauchy sequence if, given any Ïµ > 0,
there exists N âˆˆR such that |an âˆ’am| < Ïµ whenever m, n > N.
In the last section of this chapter, we shall prove the following result.
THEOREM 16E.
(GENERAL PRINCIPLE OF CONVERGENCE)
A sequence of complex num-
bers an is convergent if and only if it is a Cauchy sequence. In other words, a sequence an of complex
numbers is convergent if and only if, given any Ïµ > 0, there exists N âˆˆR such that |an âˆ’am| < Ïµ
whenever m, n > N.
Definition.
Suppose that D âŠ†C is a region. We say that a sequence of complex valued functions
an(z) is a uniform Cauchy sequence in D, if, given any Ïµ > 0, there exists N âˆˆR such that for every
z âˆˆD, |an(z) âˆ’am(z)| < Ïµ whenever m, n > N.
We have the following important result.
THEOREM 16F.
(GENERAL PRINCIPLE OF UNIFORM CONVERGENCE)
Suppose that D âŠ†
C is a region. A sequence of complex valued functions an(z) converges uniformly in D if and only if it
is a uniform Cauchy sequence in D.
Proof.
It is simple to show that uniform convergence implies uniform Cauchy. To prove the converse,
note that for every ï¬xed z âˆˆD, the sequence of complex numbers an(z) is a Cauchy sequence. It follows
from Theorem 16E that an(z) converges to a(z), say. Since an(z) is a uniform Cauchy sequence in D,
it follows that, given any Ïµ > 0, there exists N âˆˆR such that for every z âˆˆD, |an(z) âˆ’am(z)| < Ïµ
whenever m, n > N. Letting m â†’âˆ, we conclude that |an(z) âˆ’a(z)| â‰¤Ïµ whenever n > N. âƒ

THEOREM 16Q.
(CONVERGENCE THEOREM FOR POWER SERIES)
For the power series
given by (7), exactly one of the following holds:
(a) The series converges absolutely for every z âˆˆC.
(b) There exists a positive real number R such that the series converges absolutely for every z âˆˆC
satisfying |z âˆ’Î±| < R and diverges for every z âˆˆC satisfying |z âˆ’Î±| > R.
(c) The series diverges for every z Ì¸= Î±.
Sketch of Proof.
In the notation of Theorem 16P, consider
S = {r â‰¥0 : (7) converges absolutely in Dr}.
Then S contains the number 0. In view of Theorem 16P, S must be an interval with lower end-point 0,
so that S = [0, âˆ), S = {0} or there exists some positive number R such that S = [0, R) or S = [0, R].
The ï¬rst two possibilities correspond to (a) and (c) respectively, while the last possibility corresponds
to (b). âƒ

W W L Chen : Introduction to Complex Analysis
Definition.
The number R in Theorem 16Q is called the radius of convergence of the series (7). We
also say that R = 0 if case (c) occurs, and that R = âˆif case (a) occurs.
We now show that diï¬€erentiation of a power series can be carried out term by term, and that the
series so obtained converges to the derivative.
THEOREM 16R.
Suppose that the power series given by (7) has radius of convergence R > 0. Then
it represents an analytic function f(z) in the open disc D = {z : |z âˆ’Î±| < R}.
Furthermore, the
derivatives of f(z) can be obtained by diï¬€erentiating the series term by term.
Proof.
For every r < R, it follows from Theorem 16P that the series converges uniformly in the disc
Dr = {z : |zâˆ’Î±| < r}. It now follows from Theorem 16J that the series converges to an analytic function
f(z) in Dr, and the derivatives of f(z) can be obtained by diï¬€erentiating the series term by term. Since
the above holds for any r < R, the result follows. âƒ

16.6.
Cauchy Sequences
In this section, we shall prove Theorem 16E. Clearly a convergent sequence of complex numbers is
Cauchy. It remains to show that a Cauchy sequence of complex numbers is convergent.
The proof of this result usually involves the Bolzano-Weierstrass theorem which states that every
bounded sequence of complex numbers has a convergent subsequence. Here, we shall give a proof without
using the Bolzano-Weierstrass theorem.
Assume, ï¬rst of all, that the sequence an is real. Since an is a Cauchy sequence, it follows that
there exists an increasing sequence of natural numbers
